Other factors such as microRNAs and long non-coding RNAs status [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR1" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR1">1</a></span>], methylation profiles [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR117" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR117">117</a></span>] and histone marks may have to be integrated too.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">Several studies have shown that gene mutations have indeed a major impact on prognosis of myeloid diseases. This is the case in MDSs for mutations in five genes, <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">ASXL1, ETV6, EZH2, TP53</em> and <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">RUNX1</em>[<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR58" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR58">58</a></span>]. Mutations in <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">ASXL1</em> seem to be associated with an aggressive phenotype in all myeloid malignancies [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR8" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR8">8</a></span>]: they are frequent in high-risk MDSs and correlate with poor prognosis in MDSs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR118" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR118">118</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR120" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR120">120</a></span>] and with acute progression in CMML [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR36" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR36">36</a></span>], they are more frequent in myelofibrosis than in other MPNs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR37" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR37">37</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR121" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR121">121</a></span>], and characterize secondary AML. <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">DNMT3A</em> mutations are frequent in younger patients with AML and are associated with an unfavorable prognosis in MDS and AML [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR24" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR24">24</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR30" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR30">30</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR101" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR101">101</a></span>]. Among splicing gene mutations, those in <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">U2AF1</em> and <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">SRSF2</em> seem to be associated with aggressive forms of myeloid diseases and those in <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">SF3B1</em> with good prognosis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR38">38</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR39" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR39">39</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR41" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR41">41</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR42" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR42">42</a></span>]. Molecular data will allow the establishment of an upgraded index of prognosis. For example, in MDSs, it is highly conceivable that the current prognostic index used for the evaluation of the disease (IPSS), which already includes karyotypic data, can be improved by a molecular index regrouping the mutations that impact on the patients outcome [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR112" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR112">112</a></span>]. Whether <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">TET2</em> mutations are to be included is a matter of debate [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR122" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR122">122</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR124">124</a></span>]. In AML, a thorough study of 18 genes, including <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">ASXL1</em> and <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">DNMT3A</em>, proposed an updated and precise risk stratification based on gene mutations [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR125" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR125">125</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">New therapeutic targets can be found in two of the five major classes of leukemogenic genes. Following the successful use of imatinib in CML, abnormal signaling pathways associated with myeloproliferation, be it the JAK-STAT pathway [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR126" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR126">126</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR128" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR128">128</a></span>] or another pathway, represent appealing targets. Drugs targeting epigenetic modifications, i.e. epidrugs, such as histone deacetylase inhibitors and hypomethylating agents (DNMT inhibitors), are currently developed or used in clinics, and many new ones are studied in preclinical assays and clinical trials [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR1" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR1">1</a></span>]. Targeting histone methyltransferases (e.g. MLL) or lysine acetyltransferases (e.g. P300) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR129" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR129">129</a></span>] is also a promising area of development. The determination of gene mutations and their consequence on gene regulation and cell programming will help treat myeloid malignancies in providing a rationale for the use and development of new epidrugs, in directing the choice of the drug cocktails, and in allowing the design of drug delivery and the monitoring of drug response and disease progression. For example, agents directed against TET2-, IDH- and DNMT3A-associated methylation defects may represent a new area of development. To date, the use of <em xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeItalic">TET2</em> mutations status to evaluate the response to DNMT inhibitors is still debated [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR130" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR130">130</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR131" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR131">131</a></span>]. Because many mutations compromise PRC2 function drugs antagonizing this defect hold great promise.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">Proteins of two other leukemogenic classes may also serve as therapeutic targets. For example, the antitumor macrolide pladienolide targets SF3B1 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR132" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR132">132</a></span>] opening new opportunities to develop treatments against RARS. Compounds aiming at restoring a normal P53 pathway are in development [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR133" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR133">133</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR134" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR134">134</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">The existence of concomitant mutations is an incentive for combinatorial therapies; for example, therapeutic synergy may be obtained by the combined use of signaling inhibitors and epidrugs.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">Finally, the complete determination of the mutation repertoire will provide novel therapeutic targets. For some diseases, such as CMML, it is already possible to identify at least one target for nearly nine cases out of ten [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR33" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR33">33</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR36" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR36">36</a></span>]. However, the development of resistance, as observed with imatinib [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR135" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR135">135</a></span>], is a critical issue. Hopefully, target identification will allow for the development of new combinatorial strategies, such as the one based on synthetic lethality [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR136" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR136">136</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR137" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR137">137</a></span>]. If two mutations never occur together it may mean that their combined effect is deleterious. Thus, opportunities for deriving synthetic lethality drugs could stem from the observation of exclusions in mutations patterns.</p>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec14" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Conclusions</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">Thus, mutations and models (M and Ms) will help manage myeloid malignancies. The eventual comprehensive determination in any given case and at diagnosis, of the set of altered genes, underlying affected pathways and disease stage, will guide towards an optimal treatment based on an appropriate combination of drugs targeting the various affected processes of the disease. Clinically-oriented laboratories should already be preparing for that challenge. Meanwhile, there is much to mull over the M and Ms of myeloid malignancies.</p></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec15" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Authors contributions</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="Para">All authors have contributed ideas, discussions, and have participated in the writing of the manuscript. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p></div></section>
